            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b9d830757a22453994a3310efebfbdf7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '085dfa93-0d22-49a9-8f8f-918a04a8ca13'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d28073102" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A low-cost Fe-based Prussian blue analogue with increased redox potential and high energy density is reported for safe sodium-ion batteries by introducing potassium into the framework and regulating the crystal phase orientation. The unconventional and highly-reversible phase evolution is also identified, and preferred guest-ion storage sites and migration mechanism are analyzed through theoretical calculations.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd4d6e65-fc4d-4568-b279-b0df9093fc09/anie202303953-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd4d6e65-fc4d-4568-b279-b0df9093fc09/anie202303953-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/15603327-9a41-46a8-b51d-441ca9d77480/anie202303953-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd4d6e65-fc4d-4568-b279-b0df9093fc09/anie202303953-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d28073105" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) have been regarded as promising cathode materials for alkali-ion batteries owing to their high theoretical energy density and low cost. However, the high water and vacancy content of PBAs lower their energy density and bring safety issues, impeding their large-scale application. Herein, a facile “potassium-ions assisted” strategy is proposed to synthesize highly crystallized PBAs. By manipulating the dominant crystal plane and suppressing vacancies, the as-prepared PBAs exhibit increased redox potential resulting in high energy density up to ≈450 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is at the same level of the well-known LiFePO<sub>4</sub> cathodes for lithium-ion batteries. Remarkably, unconventional highly-reversible phase evolution and redox-active pairs were identified by multiple in situ techniques for the first time. The preferred guest-ion storage sites and migration mechanism were systematically analysed through theoretical calculations. We believe these results could inspire the design of safe with high energy density.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303953-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303953-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapid development of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) took place during the past decade due to the unremitting pursuit of cleaner energy storage systems (ESS).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0001_R_d28073094e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Considering the limited amount of lithium resources, however, LIBs cannot meet all energy storage demands, especially for large-scale stationary ESS. Therefore, its counterparts, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), possessing advantages such as the natural abundance of sodium, low cost, and intrinsic safety, have been regarded as one of the most promising energy systems for grid-scale applications.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0005_R_d28073094e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Many efforts have been made in electrode design (especially the cathode, which is the sodium provider in full cells) for SIBs with high energy density and long cycling life.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0008_R_d28073094e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The typical cathode materials chosen for SIBs are layered metal oxides, Prussian blue analogues (PBAs), polyanions, and organic materials, which all show great potential for practical application.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0012_R_d28073094e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Benefiting from their open framework to accommodate sodium ions, as well as advantages including no high-temperature calcination, easy production on a large scale, and nontoxicity, PBAs are considered as a class of promising materials for SIBs. The general formula for PBAs can be denoted as A<sub><i>x</i></sub>E[F(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1−<i>y</i></sub>⋅□<sub><i>y</i></sub>⋅<i>z</i> H<sub>2</sub>O (0&lt;<i>x</i>≤2, 0≤<i>y</i>&lt;1, A: alkali ions, E/F: transition metal ions, □: vacancy of [F(CN)<sub>6</sub>]). Using low-cost Fe or Mn resources and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe[CN]<sub>6</sub> as raw materials, PBAs constructed from low-priced elements possess high theoretical capacity (170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, based on two sodium-ion storage in Na<sub>2</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>], equal to the theoretical capacity of LiFePO<sub>4</sub>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0014_R_d28073094e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4b - Adv. Mater.">4b</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0018_R_d28073094e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun.">5</a></sup></span> Besides its theoretical capacity, the redox potentials of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] are around 3–3.3 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. When considering the redox potential of LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (≈3.4 V vs. Li/Li<sup>+</sup>, ≈580 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, only based on the cathode in half-cell LIBs), comparable theoretical energy density can be achieved in PBAs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0019_R_d28073094e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Taking their high theoretical energy density, low cost, and easy large-scale preparation into consideration, PBAs are highly promising for practical application. Therefore, as our group reported before, Na<sub>1.73</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]⋅3.8 H<sub>2</sub>O was synthesized by using a 100 L reactor with a 5 kg yield, showing great competitiveness for large-scale application (78 % capacity retention after 1000 cycles in a 5 A h pouch cell).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0024_R_d28073094e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Lattice defects in the framework of PBAs are inevitably generated (mainly Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4−</sup> vacancies), however, and coordinated/interstitial water will occupy these spaces, seriously impairing the electrochemical performance of PBAs for SIBs. Firstly, the formation of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> vacancies decreases the active redox pairs (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>) in the framework, resulting in a lower amount of Na<sup>+</sup> in the framework and poor energy density.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0009_R_d28073094e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3a - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">3a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0021_R_d28073094e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6b - Energy Storage Mater.">6b</a></sup></span> Secondly, because the randomly distributed vacancies break the bonds between transition metal and cyanide groups and induce the formation of a defective and distorted structure, the crystal structure is likely to be unstable, fragile, and even collapse after long-term cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0022_R_d28073094e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6c - Adv. Energy Mater.">6c</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0025_R_d28073094e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem">8</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the electronic conduction is also be affected negatively by the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> vacancies, bringing higher Ohmic polarization.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0026_R_d28073094e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span> Last but not least, the crystal water may impede Na<sup>+</sup> movement to the lattice after occupying the interstitial spaces, and the coordinated water would then dissolve in the organic electrolyte, causing side reactions and bringing safety hazards.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0026_R_d28073094e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0027_R_d28073094e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span> Although adding large amounts of chelating agents is effective for slowing down the precipitation rate for a flawless framework, the amount of unwanted water in PBAs is larger than 10 wt % no matter which synthesis method (co-precipitation, self-decomposition, ball milling) is used.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0020_R_d28073094e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0024_R_d28073094e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0025_R_d28073094e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem">8</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0028_R_d28073094e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> More importantly, for practical production in pouch cells using PBAs as cathode, our recent work shows that pouch cells are swollen after cycling when using water-containing PBAs, meaning that there are great safety hazards caused by water.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0031_R_d28073094e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span> In addition, post-treatment of PBAs by high-temperature dehydration adds to the production cost and cannot repair the defects in the framework to increase energy density. Therefore, it is crucial to decrease defects, eliminate the crystal/coordinated water in the crystal growth stage, and obtain a stable crystal structure for PBAs to promote high energy density and stable cycling in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, through regulating the crystal structure and controlling the crystal phase orientation, a series of potassium-assisted Fe-based PBAs with few defects and low water content have been prepared. After the introduction of potassium-containing raw materials, the final product (denoted as NKPB-3) displays a change in the preferred orientation of (220) plane in the framework and a more stable structure with fewer [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> vacancies, which are desirable for the sodium-ion storage and migration. In addition, the initial water content of NKPB-3 decreased dramatically (from 21.0 wt % to 7.5 wt %), improving the intrinsic safety for practical application. Benefiting from the optimized crystal structure and the introduction of potassium, NKPB-3 exhibited increased redox potentials and high initial specific capacities (147.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), resulting in high energy density (≈450 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) that is comparable to the commercial LiFePO<sub>4</sub> cathode materials for lithium-ion batteries. In addition, less voltage decay after long cycling and improved cycling stability (83.5 % capacity retention after 300 cycles) were achieved in NKPB-3 because of the pillar effect of potassium ions in its framework. There were unconventional highly reversible phase changes within cubic and monoclinic phases, the redox-active pairs were detected by in situ techniques, and the existence of different preferential sites for sodium and potassium ions was investigated through theoretical calculations. These results show that these PBAs with stable crystal structures and less water content are promising for safe SIBs with high energy density.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303953-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303953-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>A series of potassium-containing Fe-based PBAs were designed with a facile co-precipitation method. The crystal structures of sodium-based PBA without potassium (denoted as NKPB-0) and NKPB-3 (potassium-containing) were analysed using powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) by Rietveld refinement on GSAS software.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0032_R_d28073094e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> The detailed crystal information, such as atom locations and occupations, can be found in Tables S1 and S2 in the Supporting Information. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">b</a>, both NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 have the cubic phase (<i>Fm</i>-3<i>m</i>) with well-crystallized structure and no impurities.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0027_R_d28073094e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0033_R_d28073094e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Commun.">14</a></sup></span> After the introduction of potassium ions, the lattice parameter of NKPB-3 (<i>a</i>=10.0770 Å) was lower than that of NKPB-0 (<i>a</i>=10.2872 Å), meaning that the amount of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] defects was decreased and a more stable structure was obtained after the suppressed volume expansion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0034_R_d28073094e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></sup></span> Similar to NKPB-0, the low-spin/high-spin Fe ions are linked with C/N in the cyano groups in NKPB-3 (inset in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">b</a>). The XRD patterns of other potassium-containing NKPB samples were also collected, and an obvious change can be found in the intensity ratio of the (200) to the (220) plane. When adding more K<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> in the synthesis process (NKPB-4 with all K<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>), the preferred crystal plane orientation was changed from (200) to (220) plane. The intensity of (200) plane around 17.2° decreased while that of the (220) peak around 24.4° became higher, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1c</a>. The intensity ratio of the (200) to the (220) plane decreased from 1.68 to 0.69, demonstrating the effect of the substitution of potassium in the crystal structure (Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1d</a>). The type and number of elements in the framework are the primary factors influencing the relative peak intensity. Therefore, when sodium ions are replaced by potassium ions, the crystal plane orientation changes, and more (220) plane is formed. All characteristic peaks of NKPB-0 moved slightly to higher degrees after the introduction of potassium ions, meaning a smaller lattice parameter, which further confirms the result from the refinement. As discussed above, the unavoidable water, which needs to be reduced in the framework of PBAs, causes damage on cycling and is a threat to safety because of the side reactions and gas generation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0035_R_d28073094e741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano-Micro Lett.">16</a></sup></span> Benefiting from crystal optimization and low defects in its framework, it was confirmed by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) that there was less water content in NKPB-3. Obvious differences in the adsorbed water (2.2 wt %) and interstitial water (11.3 %) can be found between NKPB-0 and NKPB-3, which means that potassium introduction is an effective method to decrease the water in the PBA framework. The total water content in NKPB-3 is only 7.5 wt % with interstitial water content of 4.5 wt %, which is lower than that for most reported PBA-based materials. The low water content in NKPB-3 can reduce side reactions with organic electrolytes, provide a powerful guarantee of stable cycling, and improve intrinsic safety for practical application. Combining the result of inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (Table S3) and TGA, the chemical formulas for NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 can be calculated as Na<sub>1.86</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]⋅4.59 H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>0.28</sub>K<sub>1.55</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]⋅1.53 H<sub>2</sub>O, respectively. The higher potassium ions than the content of raw materials in the NKPB-3 are the result of more insertion of K<sup>+</sup> into the interstitial spaces of PBAs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0036_R_d28073094e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303953-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ba56c422-2370-4776-a65c-c9930c008856/anie202303953-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ba56c422-2370-4776-a65c-c9930c008856/anie202303953-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9147186e-9bce-42a6-86fc-13e8e6254fea/anie202303953-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ba56c422-2370-4776-a65c-c9930c008856/anie202303953-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303953-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303953" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Structural and morphology characterizations. a), b) Rietveld refinements of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 with inset schematic representation of the structure. Powder XRD patterns of NKPB samples (c), and corresponding intensity ratios of the (200) and (220) planes (d). e) TGA curves. f) Fourier transforms of the Fe K-edge EXAFS spectra of the samples. g) Wavelet transform of the Fe K-edge EXAFS spectrum of NKPB-3. h) HRTEM images of NKPB-3. i)–n) TEM image of NKPB-3 and corresponding element (Na, K, Fe, C, N) mapping.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), including X-ray absorption near-edge spectroscopy (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS), was conducted on the Fe K-edge, Na K-edge, and K L-edge to evaluate variations in the local electrical and structural properties of the metallic elements in the NKPB samples. (Figures S1, S2). The Fourier transforms of the Fe K-edge EXAFS spectra are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1f</a>. The spectrum shows three major peaks, with the peak positions at 1.24, 2.41, and 4.3 Å belonging to the Fe−C, Fe−N, and Fe−Fe shells, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0009_R_d28073094e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3a - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">3a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0027_R_d28073094e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span> A contour plot of the wavelet-transformed XAS of NKPB-3 is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1g</a>. <i>R</i> represents the radial distance around Fe, and <i>k</i> represents the wavenumber, which gives information on the <i>k</i>-space resolution. It appears that there is no significant difference between the wavelet transform patterns of these samples (Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1g</a>, S3), indicating that the substitution of potassium ions has not affected the local electrical and structural properties. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) was completed to demonstrate the effects of potassium substitution and crystal structure of NKPB-3. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1h</a>, the (220) plane was easily observed from the cubic structure of NKPB-3, which is the same as the result from XRD (improved intensity of the (220) plane). It is widely known that PBAs are extremely sensitive to electron beams.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0030_R_d28073094e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11b</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0031_R_d28073094e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span> NKPB-3 shows much better stability than NKPB-0 (easily turning to amorphous during focusing) when the electron beam hits, benefitting from its high heating tolerance and the stable structure of NKPB-3. The element mapping, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1i</a>–<a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">n</a>, demonstrates the successful introduction of potassium into NKPB-0 and the uniform element (Na, K, Fe, C, N) distributions of NKPB-3, which further indicates its intact crystal structure and high purity. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images indicate that both NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 show a particle diameter lower than 100 nm (Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0001">1i</a> and S4). In addition, it is worth noting that NKPB-3 is easier to agglomerate into larger and more stable secondary particles after the introduction of potassium ions (Figure S5).</p>
         
         <p>Raman spectroscopy is a useful measurement for understanding the valence state of Fe because of high sensitivity to cyano groups. Figure S6 shows the Raman spectra of the samples between 1800 and 2300 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Typically, a lower wave number means the vibration of ferrous ions with cyano groups, and a higher wave number means the vibration of ferric ions (product of the inevitable oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> because of the presence of oxygen) with cyano groups. One higher peak at 2145 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Fe<sup>2+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>3+</sup>) was found in NKPB-0, meaning more Fe<sup>3+</sup> linked with the cyano groups in the structure. The two peaks at 2079 and 2117 cm<sup>−1</sup> of NKPB-3 can be ascribed to the vibrations of Fe<sup>2+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>(3−<i>x</i>)+</sup>, exhibiting less oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> during synthesis.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0037_R_d28073094e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> The lower water content in NKPB-3 was also confirmed in the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra, showing lower transmission at 1610 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be indexed to the O−H arising from the surface water (Figure S7).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0029_R_d28073094e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11a - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">11a</a></sup></span> Similar changes in the structure of PBAs (low adsorbed/interstitial water revealed by TGA and FTIR, less oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> in Raman spectra) when changing the amount of potassium-containing raw materials, were also confirmed in NKPB-1, NKPB-2, and NKPB-4, as shown in Figure S8. When these results of potassium-containing NKPB samples are compared, the little difference between them means that it is not the number of potassium ions that brings changes, but the presence of potassium ions in the framework of PBAs. Furthermore, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were conducted on the NKPB samples in order to determine their chemical composition as well as their surface electronic states (Figure S9). K 2p orbitals were detected in the XPS spectrum of NKPB-3. For both NKPB-0 and NKPB-3, the high-resolution XPS spectrum of Fe 2p shows binding energies at 708.5 eV for Fe<sup>II</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 721.4 eV for Fe<sup>II</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. Other peaks at 710.2 eV for Fe<sup>III</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 723.5 eV for Fe<sup>III</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> were also found.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0027_R_d28073094e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0040_R_d28073094e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span> These results indicate that Fe<sup>II</sup> is mainly involved in the valence state of Fe and that Fe<sup>III</sup> plays a small role in the framework. Because of the exposure to air during the washing process, Fe<sup>III</sup> was formed by the oxidation of Fe<sup>II</sup>. This would reduce the sodium content in the framework and the specific capacity. Furthermore, to eliminate the effects of the chelating agent in the synthesis of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3, NKPB-0B and NKPB-3B were obtained by the same synthesis process without the use of a chelating agent. Similar phenomena (crystal plane orientation and structure change, low water content, less oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup>) were also found in the XRD, TGA, and Raman results for NKPB-0B or NKPB-3B, as shown in Figure S10. Therefore, combining these results mentioned above, the introduction of potassium ions into NKPB-3 alters the preferred crystal plane, decreases the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> vacancies, reduces the water content, and reduces the oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of synthesized NKPB samples was tested in 2032-type coin cells within the range of 2–4.2 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with sodium metal as the anode (Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2</a> and S11–14). As shown in the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2a</a>), the lower redox pair around 3 V can be found in both NKPB-0 (2.7/3.0 V) and NKPB-3 (2.9/3.1 V), which is mainly due to the contribution of high-spin Fe coordinated with nitrogen in cyano groups. An obvious increase in the voltages of the high redox pair (low-spin Fe coordinated with carbon in cyano groups) appears in the CV result for NKPB-3 (3.5/3.7 V) when compared with NKPB-0 (3.2/3.4 V). This means that the low-spin Fe coordinated with carbon is more likely affected after the introduction of potassium. The reason for the increase in the redox platform may be the lower potential of potassium than sodium. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2b</a>, a higher initial charging capacity is achieved in NKPB-3 (147.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) than in NKPB-0 (130.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at 15 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, due to the decreased water in the framework and fewer defects in NKPB-3. In addition, the voltage increase for the high redox pair is also shown in the galvanostatic charging/discharging curves in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2b</a>, meaning that higher energy density was achieved in NKPB-3 after the introduction of potassium. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2c</a> and Table S4, compared to previously reported PBAs,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0024_R_d28073094e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0041_R_d28073094e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span> layered transition metal oxides (LTMOs),<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0042_R_d28073094e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span> and polyanion compounds (PACs)<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0045_R_d28073094e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> as cathode material for SIBs, NKPB-3 exhibits competitive energy density up to 445.6 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is also comparable to that of LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (476 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> when the specific capacity is 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with ≈3.4 V redox potential).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0023_R_d28073094e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6d - J. Mater. Sci.">6d</a></sup></span> A similar increase in redox potential was also demonstrated in NKPB-1, NKPB-2, and NKPB-4 (Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2d</a>, S11, S12). Therefore, in order to further investigate the influence of potassium ions on the redox potential, the capacity contribution during different voltage ranges is provided in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2e</a>. From the charging curves of NKPB samples, the amount of potassium ions could not bring about any obvious changes in the redox potential. In addition, the amount of potassium ions also shows negligible effect on the capacity contribution in the high-spin Fe active region (2.9–3.1 V, ≈36 %). As discussed, the formation of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> vacancies decreases the active redox pairs in the framework, resulting in poor specific capacity. Besides, the crystal water in the framework is redox inactive, and impedes ion migration. Therefore, potassium-containing NKPB samples with fewer vacancies and less water displayed higher specific capacity. In the low-spin Fe active region (3.55–3.75 V), however, the capacity contribution from this region increased from 22.5 % to ≈28 % after the introduction of more potassium ions into the frameworks with more (220) crystal planes, further proving the effect of potassium ions on the increased redox potential and stable crystal structure. In addition, the increase in the capacity contribution of the 3.55–3.75 V region may be associated with more potassium ions entering the frameworks after adding the amount of K<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> when synthesizing. This redox region is benefited from the presence of potassium ions in the framework. More potassium ions can better extract from the framework during the charging process, leading to higher capacity contributions in this voltage range.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303953-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/30a6d1d3-c51a-4f72-9952-7c02cb5678fc/anie202303953-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/30a6d1d3-c51a-4f72-9952-7c02cb5678fc/anie202303953-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/06f13ddf-ba1d-4c2d-aa6b-a2ad52222fd3/anie202303953-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/30a6d1d3-c51a-4f72-9952-7c02cb5678fc/anie202303953-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303953-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303953" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance. a) CV curves of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) The 2<sup>nd</sup> cycle charging/discharging curves of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 at 15 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Energy density comparison between NKPB-3, NKPB-0, and other published electrodes. d) Initial charging and discharging plots of NKPB samples at 15 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Capacity contributions within different voltage ranges in the 1<sup>st</sup> charging process of NKPB samples. f) Charging/discharging curves of NKPB-3 for different cycles at 15 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Rate capacity g) and cycling performance h) of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The redox reaction in NKPB-3 was highly reversible, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2f</a>. The high redox platform was also unchanged after 50 cycles at 15 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, showing good capacity retention. The charging and discharging curves of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are shown for the different cycles in Figure S13. Obvious capacity fading occurred in NKPB-0, with the two discharging potentials decreasing by 90 and 160 mV, respectively, from the 10<sup>th</sup> cycle to the 200<sup>th</sup> cycle. Benefiting from the advantages of its optimized crystal plane orientation and stable structure with fewer defects and less water content, NKPB-3 exhibited much better cycling life, and an almost negligible voltage decrease was found at the high discharging platform. The rate performance and cycling stability of NKPB samples were also investigated (Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2g</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">h</a>, S14). The cycling performance of NKPB-0 was improved after the introduction of potassium, and NKPB-3 showed capacity retention of 83.5 % after 300 cycles with ≈100 % Coulombic efficiency (initial 5 cycles at 15 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and later at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), corresponding to only 0.055 % capacity decrease per cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0002">2h</a>). After 300 cycles, a capacity of 96.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved by NKPB-3, while only 74.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained in NKPB-0, demonstrating the effective crystal structure preservation of NKPB-3. In addition, without the use of a chelating agent, NKPB-3B also displayed an increased high redox pair compared to NKPB-0B, and better cycling performance was obtained for NKPB-3B as shown in Figure S15. From its electrochemical performance, high specific capacity and energy density were achieved because of the advantages (low water content, fewer defects, reduced oxidation of materials) derived from the optimized structure of NKPB-3.</p>
         
         <p>The reaction kinetics of these NKPB samples during ion insertion/extraction were further studied by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and CV at different scan rates. As shown in Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0003">3a</a> and S16, the calculated sodium diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7e44fe47-78ec-4dfa-8814-85e114603843/anie202303953-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
) of the NKPB samples are all within 10<sup>−12</sup>–10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, showing fast ion diffusion rates benefiting from the speed-controlled co-precipitation method.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0048_R_d28073094e1070" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> There is no obvious difference between the <i>D</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/0d72673c-d1fb-47a6-a161-c5f2445d8825/anie202303953-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
 of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 except for the low charging platform. Slow diffusion of NKPB-3 in the low charging platform may be the result of the full utilization of ferrous ion linked with N in the framework, which can be proved by the high charging capacity of NKPB-3 at this low charging platform. This means that the introduction of potassium ions into NKPB-3 changes the paths and the speed of ion diffusion by maximizing the redox pairs (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>). The EIS results on NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 (initial cell and cell after 100 cycles) are provided in Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0003">3b</a> and S17, and the inset is the fitted equivalent circuit. Two partially overlapping semicircles can be seen in the medium frequency to high frequency range, which can be ascribed to the charge-transfer resistance (R<sub>2</sub>) and the electrolyte-electrode interface film resistance (R<sub>3</sub>), indicating the charge-transfer process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0051_R_d28073094e1103" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span> A sloping line in the low frequency range represents the sodium diffusion process (Warburg impedance, W<sub>1</sub>), and R<sub>1</sub> (bulk resistance of the cell) reflects the electric conductivity of the electrodes, separator, and electrolytes. After 100 cycles, the fitting value of R<sub>2</sub> for NKPB-3 (2721 Ω) is smaller than that of NKPB-0 (4538 Ω), implying a fast charge-transfer process and stable cycling in NKPB-3. The CV tests at different scanning rates and corresponding analysis are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0003">3c</a>–<a href="#anie202303953-fig-0003">f</a>. The low redox pair induced by the high-spin Fe was selected for analysis (denoted as peaks A and A′). Lower polarization can be found in NKPB-3 when changing the scan rate from 0.1 to 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> from the gap between A and A′. In addition, a larger contribution from the diffusion-controlled process can be found in the NKPB-3 when compared with NKPB-0 when considering the sodium storage behavior in low redox pair according to the formula <i>i</i>=<i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, where <i>i</i> is the current at a peak and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are parameters (lower <i>b</i> value for NKPB-3 in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0003">3f</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303953-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a7108392-9af0-4cc3-a856-c1d0804cae4a/anie202303953-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a7108392-9af0-4cc3-a856-c1d0804cae4a/anie202303953-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ca029d6d-d229-4ce4-b61e-89ed88e685b9/anie202303953-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a7108392-9af0-4cc3-a856-c1d0804cae4a/anie202303953-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303953-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303953" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Kinetics analysis of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3. a) GITT curves of the initial cycle and the calculated <i>D</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b04cbee9-a8d5-4bf9-a7a0-4753df853af6/anie202303953-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
. b) Nyquist plots and the equivalent circuit modeling (inset) of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 electrodes after 100 cycles. CV curves at different scan rates of NKPB-0 (c) and NKPB-3 (d). e) Polarization of their high-spin Fe redox pair. f) Log (<i>i</i>) vs. log (<i>v</i>) plots from the CV results.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The structural evolution and valence change of NKPB-0 and NKPB-3 were studied to reveal the storage mechanism during cycling, as shown in Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0004">4</a>, S18, and S19. As mentioned before, the cyano groups are highly sensitive to their chemical environment, and normally, lower wave number peaks (indexed to PBAs) in Raman curves means more ferrous ion with cyano groups. As shown in Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0004">4a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0004">b</a> and S18, in situ Raman spectra were collected to detect the change of the Fe valence. Just as in the powder Raman tests, pristine NKPB-3 electrode exhibited two peaks located at 2081 and 2120 cm<sup>−1</sup>, implying the initial states of Fe<sup>2+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>(3−<i>x</i>)+</sup>, respectively. During the initial charging process, these two peaks disappeared gradually, followed by the generation of a new peak at 2157 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be ascribed to Fe<sup>3+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>3+</sup> (shaded in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0004">4b</a>). The intensity of the new peak increased and reached its highest intensity after charging to 4.2 V. This represents an almost complete change from Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup> in the NKPB-3 during the charging process.. In the following discharging process, the peak at 2157 cm<sup>−1</sup> decreased and vanished, accompanied by the appearance of the original two peaks assigned to the interconnection of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and cyano groups, demonstrating the highly reversible nature of the change for NKPB-3 in cycling. The second cycle of NKPB-3 exhibited a similar change to the Raman curves. The full conversion between Fe<sup>2+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>2+/(3−<i>x</i>)+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>−C≡N−Fe<sup>3+</sup>, which increases the degree of redox reactions, also confirms the higher specific capacity and stable cycling achieved by NKPB-3. For comparison, there was also a new shoulder peak that appeared during charging in the in situ Raman tests (Figure S18), proving the insufficient transition of Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup> in NKPB-0.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303953-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/942cb7dc-06e7-414b-bc45-6719ec9a78b5/anie202303953-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/942cb7dc-06e7-414b-bc45-6719ec9a78b5/anie202303953-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/968d7da8-fe0b-4f98-9584-f2d07b81522b/anie202303953-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/942cb7dc-06e7-414b-bc45-6719ec9a78b5/anie202303953-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303953-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303953" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Energy storage mechanism of NKPB-3. a) In situ Raman 3D colormap surface with projection. b) Line spectra (left) from in situ Raman spectroscopy and corresponding charging/discharging curves at 30 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (right). c) In situ XRD 3D colormap surface with projection. d) Line spectra (left) of in situ XRD and corresponding charging/discharging curves at 30 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (right). e) Schematic illustration of the phase transition mechanisms of NKPB-3. f)–h) Enlarged in situ XRD results for NKPB-3.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In situ XRD tests were used to analyse the phase changes during the charging and discharging process of NKPB-3 and NKPB-0, as shown in the three-dimensional (3D) colormap surfaces with projection in Figures <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0004">4c</a>–<a href="#anie202303953-fig-0004">h</a> and S19, respectively. Typically, most reported PBAs for SIBs exhibited initial cubic, monoclinic, or rhombohedral phase, and they usually underwent reversible phase changes when cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0024_R_d28073094e1281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0038_R_d28073094e1281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18a - Nano Lett.">18a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0052_R_d28073094e1281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span> As summarized in Table S5, many PBAs changed into tetragonal phase with no alkali ions or cubic phase when charging to the final voltage, and they came back to their initial phase. A unique phase change took place in NKPB-3, however, which has never been reported before, when it was cycled within 2–4.2 V at 30 mA g<sup>−1</sup> during the first two cycles. The initial characteristic peaks of cubic NKPB-3 can be found in the initial electrodes except for some peaks that overlapped with the background (such as low peak (420) and Al foil, low peak (422) and BeO). During the initial charging process, new weak peaks located at 26.6°, 28.4°, 31.0°, and 31.9° appeared, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0004">4c</a>, which are likely the characteristic peaks of monoclinic phase, implying a phase transition from cubic to monoclinic phase. The (400) and (600) peaks also moved to lower angle, corresponding to the lattice distortion because of the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> in the initial framework of NKPB-3. Importantly, the peaks that were indexed to monoclinic phase fully vanished when discharging to 2 V, and the (400) and (600) peaks came back to their initial location, demonstrating the highly reversible nature of the change in crystal phase of NKPB-3. In addition, a similar phenomenon was also found in the second cycle for NKPB-3. The in situ XRD patterns of NKPB-0 exhibited the common phase change between cubic to tetragonal phase, and the new peaks in NKPB-3 could not be found in the XRD pattern of NKPB-0, as shown in Figure S19. Therefore, after the introduction of potassium ions, NKPB-3 exhibited a unique reversible phase change from initial cubic to monoclinic phase (charged state), which may be the result of low defect content in the framework, different dominant crystal planes, and the large ionic radius of K<sup>+</sup> ions, some of which still remain in the framework after fully charging.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0036_R_d28073094e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> This unique phase transition in PBAs may also help to achieve stable cycling, as obtained in NKPB-3.</p>
         
         <p>To further understand the mechanisms of energy storage and migration in the PBA framework that contribute to outstanding performance, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0005">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0005">b</a>, four possible Na<sup>+</sup> interstitial positions are depicted: 8c (body-centered), 24d (face-centered), 32f(n) (displaced from 8c sites toward an N-coordinated corner), and 32f(c) (displaced from 8c sites toward a C-coordinated corner) with Wyckoff notations in cubic units.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0021_R_d28073094e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6b - Energy Storage Mater.">6b</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0030_R_d28073094e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11b</a></sup></span> Typically, the largest free space is located at the 8c site, while the smallest is found at the 24d site.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0055_R_d28073094e1313" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the defect-rich crystal structure would have large interstices, which are typically filled by coordinated water molecules as a result of the loss of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> octahedra.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0056_R_d28073094e1319" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Energy">27</a></sup></span> This is also the reason why NKPB-0 has a large amount of water in its framework while NKPB-3 has a limited amount. It was previously reported that the Na<sup>+</sup> would be difficult to extract from the water clusters in the defect-rich crystal structure if they were inserted at the interstices occupied by water molecules.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303953-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0031_R_d28073094e1324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0033_R_d28073094e1324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Commun.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202303953-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303953-bib-0056_R_d28073094e1324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Energy">27</a></sup></span> This resulted in a low specific capacity (130.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) when NKPB-0 with more defects was used as a cathode material for SIBs. The adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) in both Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and K<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> was calculated by DFT in order to determine the relative stability of Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> occupation in different active sites (Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0005">5c</a>). Based on Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, the 24d site exhibits the lowest <i>E</i><sub>a</sub>, whereas the 8c site displays the highest <i>E</i><sub>a</sub>. Lower values of <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> mean a lower energy barrier at the site, which makes the process of adsorption easier. Thus, sodium ions prefer to occupy the 24d site in Na-based PBAs instead of any of the other three sites. Interestingly, the 24d site <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> of K<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> presents a higher value than other three sites. It is more beneficial for potassium ions to occupy sites with large free spaces due to their large radius. Consequently, after the introduction of potassium ions, the framework of PBAs is slightly altered, as shown by the XRD results. Considering the symmetry of the PBA structure, when Na<sup>+</sup> or K<sup>+</sup> migrate through the body center along an axis, a higher energy barrier is calculated in K<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, suggesting a slow diffusion process due to the large radius (Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0005">5d</a>–<a href="#anie202303953-fig-0005">f</a>). Furthermore, the redox potentials of Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, KNaFeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, and K<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> were calculated (Figure <a href="#anie202303953-fig-0005">5g</a>–<a href="#anie202303953-fig-0005">i</a>). In the mathematical framework, all calculated redox potentials are above 3.5 V. With an increase in potassium content, the redox potentials of the framework change dramatically. The second charging potential for KNaFeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> is 4.19 V, which is much higher than that of Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> (3.63 V). This is in line with the results of the experiment, which showed that the redox potential went up after the introduction of potassium ions into the framework. In this regard, the introduction of potassium ions is advantageous for the fabrication of PBA electrode materials for SIBs that have a high energy density and long cycling life.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303953-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f11aaaf-37aa-44a7-b4c2-4b218463c307/anie202303953-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f11aaaf-37aa-44a7-b4c2-4b218463c307/anie202303953-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6e8c7537-f330-4a82-9d8c-1e8f907a1b34/anie202303953-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f11aaaf-37aa-44a7-b4c2-4b218463c307/anie202303953-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303953-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303953" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>DFT calculations. a) Primitive crystal structure with four possible sodium-ion storage positions. b) Illustration of four sodium-ion storage positions. c) Adsorption energies when occupying different cation interstitial sites.          </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303953-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303953-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we have successfully synthesized a series of low-cost Fe-based PBAs with low water content, reduced crystal defects, and high alkali ion content in their frameworks via a facile “potassium-ion assisted” strategy. NKPB-3 displays a preference to the orientation of (220) crystal plane and a more stable structure with fewer [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> vacancies. The initial water content of NKPB-3 decreased dramatically (decreased from 21.0 wt % to 7.5 wt % total), improving its intrinsic safety for practical application. Benefitting from its optimized crystal structure and the pillar effect of potassium ions in its framework, high energy density (≈450 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) and stable cycling were achieved in NKPB-3 because of the increased redox potentials, high initial specific capacity (147.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and negligible voltage decay after cycling. The unique phase transition between cubic and monoclinic phases as evidenced by in situ XRD for the first time and the change in redox-active pairs from in situ Raman spectroscopy were highly reversible, proving the effective improvement of its electrochemical performance after the rational design of its crystal structure. In addition, different preferential sites for sodium (24d) and potassium (8c) ions and the high redox potentials of NKPB-3 were proved through theoretical calculations. These results show that PBAs with a stable crystal structure and less water content are promising for high energy density and safe alkali-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303953-sec-0004">
